Preface
This thesis was written as part of the requirements to obtain the Masters of Science
degree in Biomedical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The presented work is the product of a 30 ECTS points project, and was carried out
between the 1st of April 2022 and the 8th of August 2022. The project was completed
in cooperation with the Mignot Lab at Stanford University.
The objective of the Thesis was to render the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire (ASQ)
dataset utilizable for machine learning application and implement the ASQ to classify
Obstructive sleep apnea (OSA) from the apnea-hypopnea index (AHI) severity scale.
, August 8, 2022
Giorgio Camillo Ricciardiello Mejia (s202327)
==PAGE:6==
ii
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The purpose of this Thesis is to adapt the ASQ tabular dataset, for the implementa-
tion of a Machine Learning (ML) algorithm to classify OSA.
In reference to the American Academy of Sleep Medicine (AASM) scoring manual,
the gold standard measure for OSA diagnosis is through a costly and time-consuming
Polysomnography (PSG) sleep study. Where diﬀerent respiratory events are mea-
sured and quantiﬁed, obtaining a ﬁnal AHI score. To ease the diagnosis costs, sleep
questionnaires had been utilized as a subjective measure for OSA and other sleep
disorders.
The widely used eXtreme Gradient Boosting (XGB) [1] is selected as the learning
algorithm. With decision tree boosting properties, suitable for the amalgamation of
categorical, ordinal, and continuous variables of the ASQ. Moreover, the extensive
selection of hyperparameters for model tuning in XGB makes the learning process
adaptable for the dataset. To achieve the best possible performance metrics. The
result of diﬀerent hyperparameter conﬁgurations was tested with a Bayesian Opti-
mization algorithm provided by the Optuna library [2].
From the combination of four diﬀerent datasets Stanford Technology Analytics
and Genomics in Sleep (STAGES) (n = 1767), Ariweave (n = 526), Balance (n = 1776),
and Reﬂex (n = 815). The models was trained by dividing the dataset into train (70%),
validation (20%) and test (10%). Three diﬀerent models were considered based on
the subjects gender. Both gender model, solely male gender model, and solely female
model. The target (AHI) class imbalance was accounted for at the creation of each
split to preserve an approximated balance of target cases. Feature selection from the
pre-processed dataset with 412 predictors was implemented with the use of mutual
information (MI), and Analysis of variance (ANOVA). The 40 most signiﬁcant pre-
dictors based on their importance score measure.
On the test set, the averaged Area under the curve (AUC) of the three best
models for the both gender model, male model, and female model were 0.605, 0.654,
and 0.634, respectively. In terms of accuracy the both gender model, male model,
and female model obtained 0.408, 0.487, 0.446. Furthermore, the Fscore for the both
gender model, male model, and female model were 0.395, 0.399, and 0.46. The models

demonstrated substandard discriminatory characteristic for the classiﬁcation task as
a four-class problem.

Decision surface of a decision tree trained on pairs of features dem_0110

with AHI as a binary class. Decision boundaries are all constructed from
constant base learners, consequently, the boundary decision is linear be-
tween the R j regions, with j = 0,...,T. For visual purposes, two continuous
predictors were selected.

5.2
The ASQ and PSG study can be done on diﬀerent dates. To avoid a wide time gap
between the ASQ and AHI. The number of days (∆) between the two measures is
utilized as a threshold parameter for subject selection. Subjects with a ∆higher
than the threshold is removed, reducing the number of subjects, as presented in
the column Remain. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

cleansing. The table depicts the percentage of questions that had a frequency
response given on the left side of the table.
A frequency range percentage in
the range [100,95] indicates the question (column) contains between [0,5][%] of

==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
This chapter is dedicated to the Thesis introduction, project statement, objective goals,
and Thesis outline
1.1
Introduction
Most individuals can comprehend the negative consequences a single ﬁtful night may
cause on the next day e.g. cognitive impairments, and anxiety. For patients diagnosed
with OSA, accounting for > 5% in the danish adult population [3]; restless nights tend
to persist, worsening the gravity of the side eﬀects of recurrent ﬁtful nights. Moreover,
OSA is associated with the presence of comorbidities such as cardiovascular disease,
hypertension, diminished quality of life, and risk factors such as motor vehicle acci-
dents [4].
At the basic level, the diagnosis of OSA might appear straightforward to iden-
tify with initial sleep complaints. Although, this basic method lacks speciﬁcity and
does not suﬃciently capture the complexities of the sleep problem. Consequently,
polysomnographic measures are used to detect the AHI. However, due to the severity
and requirement of polysomnographic studies, the diagnosis and late treatment of
OSA have exorbitant monetary costs [5].
Therefore, the introduction of rigorous sleep questionnaires are studied and imple-
mented to ease the diagnosis of OSA [6, 7, 8, 9]. The comprehensive Alliance Sleep
Questionnaire (ASQ) is a self-report measure of a patients sleep quality perception
and targets subjective symptoms and consequences of sleep disturbances. However,
the AHI remains a polysomnographic measure.
In an eﬀort to provide the AHI directly from the ASQ. This Thesis, adapts the
ASQ for the implementation of a ML algorithm, to classify OSA as a four-class prob-
lem. With AHI ranges deﬁned by the AASM scoring manual [10].
==PAGE:30==
2
1 Introduction
1.2
Project Statement
The primary project goal is to implement a machine learning algorithm able to exploit
the tabular ASQ dataset to classify the AHI into the four severity indexes. The project
will be carried out by solving the smaller objectives listed below:
• Examine the medical literature on sleep breathing disorders and which are the
implemented guidelines for the diagnosis
• Conduct a thorough literature overview of studies utilizing sleep questionnaires
and EMR with the use of ML to target AHI.
• Gain the required clearance and certiﬁcate to access and use the Stanford Re-
search Repository (STARR) tools for data retrieval.
– Complete 19 graded courses from the Collaborative IRB Training Initiative
(CITI) program.
– Complete the Stanford University Module (ID 750).
• Design a preprocessing pipeline for managing missing values of the ASQ and
render the ASQ utilizable for a ML algorithm.
• Select a ML algorithm capable of learning from a tabular dataset containing
diﬀerent data types.
• Implemented data reduction methods to identify the most signiﬁcant predictors.
• train, validate, optimize, and test three diﬀerent models based on the subjects
gender

Thesis Outline
The main chapters of this thesis are structured as follows
Chapter 2 introduces the clinical background of sleep and its relation with the
body’s breathing mechanism. The diﬀerent medical guidelines for the diagnosis of
OSA are stated, with the main characters through PSG, and complaints. The dif-
ferent sections that construct the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire (ASQ) are brieﬂy
explained.
Chapter 3 provides a state-of-the-art review for ML studies with the utilization
of sleep questionnaires to predict the AHI or OSA.
Chapter 4 provides an overview of all used data, which is relevant for analyzing
the main patient characteristics, AHI distribution, and how was ensemble in a uniﬁed

==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2
Clinical Background
This chapter gives a brief overview of how the breathing response of the body behaves
to maintain arterial partial pressures of carbon dioxide and oxygen at relatively con-
stant levels during sleep. Followed by the introduction of the clinical manuals used to
deﬁne, identify, and classify diﬀerent sleep breathing disorders. Lastly, the chapter
introduces the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire as a screening tool to target subjective
sleep symptoms with elf-report measures the patient’s sleep quality perception
2.1
Normal Human Sleep: An Overview
Sleep is a behavioral state where a complex combination of physiological and behav-
ioral processes occur. Generally, sleep is accompanied by behavioral quiescence, closed
eyes, and rhythmic physiologic parameters. Within sleep, two separate stages are de-
ﬁned to diﬀerentiate measurable physiologic parameters (e.g. Electroencephalography
(EEG) activity, Electrooculogram (EOG), Electromyography (EMG)):
non-REM
(NREM) and rapid eye movement (REM). A healthy sleep progression is marked
by a consistent schedule of about 8 hours per night, and the average period of the
NREM-REM cycle is about 90 to 110 minutes [11].
The onset of sleep is through the NREM stage and is conventionally subdivided
into four further stages based on the EEG activity.
During this stage, the EEG
captures synchronous cortical activity, including K-complexes, sleep spindles, and
high-voltage slow-wave associated with minimal physiological activity and low mus-
cle tonus. The aforementioned NREM stages are characterized by diﬀerent duration
and stimulus intensity to produce arousal [12, 13, 14]. Once completed (80 to 100
minutes), the sleep oscillates into the REM stage. By contrast, the EEG captures
desynchronized activity, muscles are in an atonic phase. The distinction of tonic ver-
sus phasic is based on short events e.g. eye movements, which occur at intermittent
occurrences, and are separated by episodes of relative quiescence [11].
==PAGE:34==
6
2 Clinical Background
2.2
Cardiovascular Physiology and Sleep
The cardiovascular autonomic nervous system ensures the required cardiac output to
vital organs through continuous and rapid adjustments of blood pressure (BP) and
heart rate (HR) [15]. Sleep and circadian states alter the characteristics of HR and
BP, as they have a diurnal behavior characterized by a reduction during nighttime.
Diﬀerent autonomic cardiovascular patterns dominate during NREM vs REM
sleep. The NREM sleep cycle is characterized by a period of autonomic stability,
with a near-sinusoidal modulation of HR variation recognized as “normal respiratory
sinus arrhythmia” with a reduction in the cardiac output [16]. A signiﬁcant reduction
in BP and systolic BP is characterized as NREM progresses from stages N1 to N3.
Suggesting a reduction in cardiac and peripheral sympathetic activity [17, 18, 19].
The decrease in cardiac output results from reductions in stroke volume and heart
rate.
By contrast, REM sleep is a state of autonomic instability, with ﬂuctuations be-
tween sympathetic and parasympathetic inﬂuences, causing sudden changes in BP
and HR [20]. The average HR and BP are higher during REM than in NREM sleep.
The reﬂexive cardiovascular changes caused during breathing pauses, and increases
in breathing rates, and serve as a compensatory mechanism to normalize arterial BP
[16]. These physiologic patterns can be altered by sleep breathing disorders, with
important implications for cardiovascular health.
Moreover, patients with a sleep breathing disorder and heart disease are at higher
risk for cardiac events; particularly during REM sleep when an increase in sympathetic
tone manifests. Injuries to the central autonomic regularity sites and upper airway
obstruction are common in patients with OSA [16].
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Sleep-Related Breathing Disorders: Clinical Approach
7
2.3
Sleep-Related Breathing Disorders: Clinical
Approach
The diagnostic criteria and deﬁnition for sleep-related breathing disorders in adults
are provided by the ICSD3 [21]. Under the ICSD3 deﬁnition, a disorder refers to a
disease process for which a cause is known. Whereas, a syndrome is an amalgamation
of symptoms.
Below are listed the ICSD3 sleep-related breathing disorder classiﬁcation in adults.
• Obstructive Sleep Apnea Disorders
– Obstructive Sleep Apnea, Adults
• Central Sleep Apnea Disorders
– Central Sleep Apnea with Cheyne-Stokes Breathing
– Central Sleep Apnea Due to a Medical Disorder without Cheyne-Stokes Breath-
ing
– Central Sleep Apnea Due to High Altitude Periodic Breathing
– Central Sleep Apnea Due to a Medication or Substance
– Primary Central Sleep Apnea
– Treatment-Emergent Central Sleep Apnea
• Sleep-Related Hypoventilation Disorders
– Obesity-Hypoventilation Syndrome
– Idiopathic Central Alveola hypoventilation
– Sleep-Related Hypoventilation Due to Medication or Substance
– Sleep-Related Hypoventilation Due to a Medical Disorder
• Sleep Related Hypoxemia Disorders
– Sleep-Related Hypoxemia
The diagnosis of adult OSA accounts for obstructive and mixed apneas, hypopneas,
and respiratory eﬀort-related arousals (RERAs) [22]. Clinically, OSA is characterized
by the occurrence of frequent episodes of partial or complete upper airway obstruction
during sleep causing-but not solely, oxygen desaturation [23]. Novel OSA symptoms
and signs include excessive daytime sleepiness, morning headaches, loud snoring, and
gasping at night [24].
Conversely to OSA, Central sleep apnea (CSA) is deﬁned by the cessation of air-
ﬂow without respiratory eﬀort, both thoracoabdominal excursions and oronasal ﬂow
are absent [25]. As previously listed, in the deﬁnition of the ICSD3, CSA includes six
heterogeneous adult syndromes with a common ventilatory pattern [25].
==PAGE:36==
8
2 Clinical Background
Sleep-Related Hypoventilation Disorders describe breathing that is too slow or
shallow and refers to the alveolar ventilation during sleep. Hypoventilation occurs
when a reduced amount of air enters the alveoli, resulting in low oxygen and increased
carbon dioxide in blood levels; disturbing sleep quality. The disorder is classiﬁed into
four sub-types by the ICSD3, as previously listed.
Similar to sleep hypoventilation disorder, sleep-Related Hypoxemia Disorder man-
ifests with a low level of oxygen in the blood. Although, it diﬀers with no signiﬁcant
increase in carbon dioxide levels in the blood.
In order to better quantify OSA, the AASM scoring manual [10] deﬁnes the AHI
as the number of apneas and hypopneas per hour of sleep [22]. The AHI is then
used as diagnostic criteria for OSA as presented in Table 2.3, where OSA severity is
classiﬁed from Normal to Severe. In contrast, the ICSD3 does not deﬁne the AHI
and the respiratory event index. Consequently, both diagnostic criteria diﬀer. The
AASM requires the presence of symptoms and comorbidities, and at least ﬁve or more
obstructive respiratory events per hour of sleep. The AASM also has as satisfying
criteria, the demonstration of ﬁfteen or more respiratory events per hour of sleep,
independently of symptoms and comorbidities. In the case of obstructive respiratory
events in the range of [5, 15] (Mild Sleep Apnea), the risk of overdiagnosing is more
probable in an asymptomatic patient. The diagnostic criteria for OSA can be ob-
served and compared between the recommended manuals in Table 2.3.
AHI
Rating
< 5
Normal (No Sleep Apnea)
5−15
Mild Sleep Apnea
15−30
Moderate Sleep Apnea
> 30
Severe Sleep Apnea
Table 2.1: The Apnea-Hypopnea Index (AHI), as with the separate Apnea Index
and Hypopnea Index, is calculated by dividing the number of events by the number
of hours of sleep.
==PAGE:37==

In addition, the AASM scoring manual provides the diagnostic criteria deﬁnition
for the scoring of sleep and associated events as hypopnea and respiratory eﬀort-
related arousal, and are deﬁned as follows [22]:
==PAGE:38==
10
2 Clinical Background
Hypopnea
1A. Score a respiratory event as hypopnea if all of the following criteria are met
(a) The peak signal excursions of drop by ≥30% of pre-event baseline using
a hypopnea sensor.
(b) The duration of the ≥30% drop in the signal excursion is ≥10 seconds.
(c) There is ≥3% oxygen desaturation from pre-event baseline, or the event
is associated with an arousal
1B. Score a respiratory event as a hypopnea if all of the following criteria are met:
(a) The peak signal excursions of drop by ≥30% of pre-event baseline using
a hypopnea sensor.
(b) The duration of the ≥30% drop in the signal excursion is ≥10 seconds.
(c) There is a ≥4% oxygen desaturation from the pre-event baseline.
Respiratory Effort-Related Arousal
If there is a sequence of breathing lasting ≥10 seconds with a noticeable increase of
respiratory eﬀort and the sensor does not meet the criteria for OSA or hypopnea.
2.4
Polysomnography
The gold standard for measuring physiologic parameters during sleep in medical prac-
tice is the use of the PSG. A PSG is a systematic process used to measure multiple
physiological changes during a sleep study. This test records brain activity with EEG,
eye movements with EOG, muscle activity with EMG, heart rate variability with
Electrocardiography (ECG), blood oxygen saturation with peripheral pulse oximetry,
and respiratory eﬀort and airﬂow.
2.4.1
Procedure
PSGs are primarily used to diagnose sleep-related breathing disorders (SRBDs), in-
cluding OSA, sleep-related hypoventilation/hypoxia, and central sleep apnea [26].
The diagnosis guidance of OSA has been established by the AASM and the ICSD3.
All EEG channels are mainly utilized to determine the ﬁve sleep-wake stages with
the additional measurements from the EOG and chin EMG.
==PAGE:39==
2.4 Polysomnography
11
2.4.1.1
Airflow and respiratory effort
Airﬂow is measured with the use of two qualitative sensors. The oronasal thermal
sensor (thermistor) and the nasal pressure transducer. The oronasal thermal sensor
detected changes in temperature caused by inspired vs expired airﬂow. Whereas the
nasal pressure transducer detects pressure changes and identiﬁes hypopneas. The use
of the nasal pressure transducer in conjunction with the thermistor yields a more
sensitive measure of airﬂow restriction [27].
respiratory inductance plethysmography (RIP) sensors are utilized to measure
inspiratory air-ﬂow limitation. RIP consists of two diﬀerent analog voltage signals
generated from two bands, respectively. The transducers bands are placed around
the patient’s rib cage and abdomen. A signal decrease of the RIP sum channels in-
dicates a respiratory disturbance [28]. An alternative, and complementary sensor for
monitoring respiratory eﬀort published in 2007 by the AASM, is the intercostal/di-
aphragmatic EMG [10].
2.4.1.2
Oxygen saturation
Pulse oximetry is an active noninvasive measurement of the oxygen saturation in the
blood. It uses small light emitting diode (LED)s - with red and infrared frequen-
cies, facing a photodiode through a translucent extremity of the patient, such as the
ﬁngertip or earlobe. The photodiode captures the diﬀerence of light from the not ab-
sorbed red and infrared frequencies. Infrared light is mainly absorbed by oxygenated
hemoglobin whereas, red light is mainly absorbed by deoxygenated hemoglobin [29].
This physical measure provides an estimate of oxygen saturation of the arterial blood.
2.4.1.3
Electrocardiography
With the use of electrodes placed on the body’s surface. The ECG measures the
electrical activity that travels through the heart’s conduction pathways. Each ECG
complex represents a speciﬁc section of the heart’s myocardial action potentials, con-
veying continuous information about the heart’s depolarization and re-polarization.
In 1984, Guillemiault et al. published the ﬁrst study of the heart response to the
eﬀects of sleep apnea [30]. The study concludes on how the ECG can be utilized as a
screening tool for sleep apnea. The 400 sleep-apnoeic patients, demonstrated at sleep
apnea onset progressive bradycardia, followed by abrupt tachycardia on resumption
of breathing.
==PAGE:40==
12
2 Clinical Background
2.5
Alliance Sleep Questionnaire
The self-report measures the patient’s sleep quality perception and targets subjective
symptoms and consequences of sleep disturbances.
The ASQ is a comprehensive,
on-line1 sleep questionnaire designed in collaboration with Harvard University, U
Penn, U Madison-Wisconsin, and St Louis [6]. It uses branching logic to asses sleep
symptoms and generate a “Physician Summary Report” prior to the patient’s clinic
appointment. The ﬁnal goal of the ASQ is to reduce the risk of overlooking comorbid
disorders.
The ASQ has been a long-lasting standard of care at the Stanford Sleep Clinic
and has been validated against medical diagnoses of Restless legs syndrome (RLS),
narcolepsy, and OSA [6, 7, 8, 9]. Table 2.5 depicts the diﬀerent questionnaire sections
of the ASQ.
• Schedule
• Demographics
• Medical history
• Medications
• Family history
• Bedtime habits
• Personal health infor-
mation
• Social History
• Apnea
• Sleepiness
• Insomnia
• Restless leg syndrome
• Circadian rhythms
• Narcolepsy
• Parasomnia
• Quality of life
• Depression
• Anxiety
• Fatigue
• Satisfaction survey
Table 2.3: Sections of the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire
The ASQ is not disease-speciﬁc and attempts to expand the inclusion of all
sleep disorders by reviewing a wide variety of sleep habits and targeting speciﬁc-
related questions. The ASQ uses an amalgamation of variables such as Electronic
health record (EHR), Epworth Sleepiness Scale (ESS), Patient Health Questionnaire-
9 (PHQ-9) for depression, Generalized anxiety disorder-7 (GAD-7) [31, 32] for anxi-
ety, Fatigue Severity Scale (FSS) [33, 34], reduced morningness eveningness question-
naire (r-MEQ) [35] for circadian rhythms, etc.., to accurately incorporate all possible
patient subjective complaints and better report a sleep disorder.
1https://oursleep-demo.stanford.edu
==PAGE:41==
2.5 Alliance Sleep Questionnaire
13
The ESS is a self-administered questionnaire to assess the daytime sleepiness of
patients with 8 questions. Each item is rated on a 0-4 scale with a total score of
24. The score is able to discriminate between various diagnostic groups such as OSA,
idiopathic hypersomnia, and narcolepsy.
The ESS does not include the patient’s
subjective criteria of sleep quality or the patient’s sleep distribution. The ESS espe-
cially distinguishes between reports of dozing behavior in a wide variety of activities.
Consequently, limiting the ESS to distinguish which sleep disorder has caused any
particular average sleep propensity in daily life [36].
The Multivariable Apnea Prediction (MAP) index is a sleep questionnaire screen-
ing tool for OSA [37]. The MAP index utilizes the BMI, and various self-reported
OSA-related questions such as loud snore, frequent wakening, snorting gasping, exces-
sive daytime sleepiness driving, age, and sex. The predictive probability of the MAP
index has demonstrated high sensitivity and speciﬁcity in various studies [38, 39, 40].
In contrast to the MAP methodology, the Pittsburgh Sleep Quality Index (PSQI)
consists of 19 self-rated questions and ﬁve-bed partner-rated questions. Speciﬁcally,
assess sleep quality and disturbances in a longitudinal manner by administering the
PSQI on two occasions separated by approximately a 1-month time interval [41]. A
wide variety of subjective factors relating to sleep latency, sleep duration, sleep qual-
ity, and frequency and severity of speciﬁc sleep-related problems [42].
==PAGE:42==
14
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 3
State of the Art
This chapter provides a review of current automatic state-of-the-art methods for OSA
prediction and classiﬁcation based on sleep questionnaire and EMR data
In-laboratory PSG is the accepted gold standard for diagnosing sleep disorders.
However, PSGs are a costly and time-consuming procedure. Considering the high
prevalence of OSA and its impact on health and quality of life, screening question-
naires such as the ASQ, Snoring, Tiredness, Observed apnea, blood Pressure, Body
mass index, Age, Neck circumference and Gender (STOP-Bang), Insomnia Severity
Index (ISI), Athens Insomnia Scale (AIS), ESS, Berlin questionnaire (BQ), PSQI,
FSS, etc.., with related sleep questions are implemented. Therefore, to prioritize pa-
tients with suspected OSA, several studies have been proposed in the sleep-medicine
literature with the objective of automatically predicting AHI from non-PSG features.
This chapter aims to enunciate these methods and give an overview of the particu-
larities of each study. An overview builds the pillars for decision-making on how to
best address the objective’s main problems by increasing the knowledge in this ﬁeld
of research and states the benchmarks of the best models.
3.1
Automatic Apnea-Hypopnea Severity Predictor
There have been published several methods for automatic detection of OSA from
non-PSG features. A summary of these can be found below in Table 3.1.
Ustun et al. (2016) studies the prediction of OSA as a binary class problem with-
out directly predicting the AHI. An OSA is deﬁned when AHI > 5 or disturbance
index (RDI) > 10 (even if AHI was < 5) [43]. Self-reporter data before PSG are used
as predictors and contain a series of patient’s comorbidities, self-reported symptoms
of various sleep disorders, STOP-Bang, and BQ.
The study employs its own designed ML algorithm named “Supersparse Linear Inte-
ger Models” [44]. The algorithm is designed as a scoring system that is both accurate
and interpretable for classiﬁcation models. The scoring system is a linear binary clas-
siﬁer with scoring weights for each independent predictor. The result of the study
remains rather limited with a recall of 64.2% and a speciﬁcity of 77%. Although, it ob-
tained state-of-the-art metrics in comparison to other studies with the same dataset.
Moreover, the author demonstrates how OSA can be automatically diagnosed with
==PAGE:44==
16
3 State of the Art
easier-to-acquire factors.
The work of Wu et al. (2017) [45], proposes a new AHI prediction method us-
ing easier-to-acquire predictors, with a total of 150 subjects and a ﬁve-fold cross-
validation scheme with stratiﬁed sampling based on the OSA severity by considering
a two-class problem, and an neural fuzzy evaluation system (NFES) [46]. With the
utilization of stepwise regression, among the eight predictors, BMI, the diﬀerence in
systolic BP, and ESS were the most signiﬁcant and used in the NFES to predict AHI.
An advantage of the NFES is the model interpretability with direct domain knowl-
edge.
Conversely, the NFES is optimized by minimizing the error function E =
1
2
¡
AHI−AHId¢, for high AHI in the severe category may cause a positive bias in
estimating the AHI for the normal category [45]. A remedy to the study is the inclu-
sion of a more balanced dataset. The average accuracy, sensitivity, and speciﬁcity of
the NFES were 75.6%, 77.2%, and 75.0%, respectively.
Similarly, Young Jae Kim et al. (2021) [47], with a binary OSA classiﬁer problem,
presents a study with 285 participants and deﬁne the cut-oﬀfor OSA as AHI≥5/h.
From 92 clinical variables, including questionnaire answers, ESS, BQ, PSQI, and
FSS in addition to easy-to-acquire predictors from body measures. Seven signiﬁcant
predictors were selected by permutation feature selection with a random shuﬄe [48,
49]. The most signiﬁcant predictors were: waist circumference, the loudness of snor-
ing from the BQ, FSS total score, subnasale to stomion, falling asleep from the BQ,
hypertension, and snoring from BQ. Four ML models were trainer: Support Vector
Macchines (SVM), Logistic Regression (LR), Random Forest (RF), XGB with accu-
racies: 83.33%, 75%, 78.57%, 75%. All models obtained acceptable performances in
terms of accuracy.
With a class imbalance of OSA (n=149) and no-OSA (n=46), the models present
a high bias towards the non-OSA groups. Although, the study highlight how the
correct selection of seven predictors is capable of presenting acceptable performances.
T3.1he metrics of all models are presented. As the number of predictors increases
SVM diminishes in performance as the boundaries between clusters overlap [47].
The study by Jiayan Huo et al. (2022) uses the AASM scoring manual to deﬁne
OSA based on the AHI severity but limits the classiﬁer with a binary problem by
grouping minimal and mild as low-risk classes, and moderate and severe as a high-
risk class [50]. Risk factors associated with OSA easily accessible for a questionnaire
were used as predictors for the model with n = 676 patients.
In addition, the mutual information score between each risk factor and binary AHI
was used for feature reduction by selecting the top 50% (n=6) predictors (BMI, gender,
neck girth, snoring loudness, hypertension, and age). Consequently, the BASH-GN
remains as a 6-item questionnaire.
Eight diﬀerent algorithms were tested for the
classiﬁer model, and Logistic regression had the best AUROC performance in both
subtypes. In comparison to the STOP-Bang, Berlin, Four-Variable, and ESS ques-
tionnaires. However, a binary classiﬁer for OSA with a cut-oﬀvalue of 15 AHI may

Table 3.1: Methods for automatic detection of AHI with the use of questionnaires and/or

The described methods proposed in the literature revolve around ﬁrstly reducing
the target complexity by ﬁxing the AHI into a binary-class problem for OSA classiﬁ-
cation. To the author’s knowledge, no relevant studies have worked with a multi-class
or regression problem for the prediction of AHI with non-PSG predictors.
Secondly, pertinent feature extraction is realized by Young Jae Kim et al. (2021),
whereby selecting seven of the 92 clinical variables, superior metrics were obtained
in comparison to the other presented studies. Similarly, in the studies of Ustun et
al. (2016), Wu et al. (2017), and Jiayan Huo et al. (2022), few relevant predictors
are necessary to obtain the published metrics. Predictors such as BMI, waist circum-
ference, ESS, and BQ questionnaires were stated as the most relevant predictors, all
related to OSA from domain knowledge.
An additional observation is the limited number of samples in contrast to the
study of Ustun et al.
(2016).
It is reasonable to suggest how the small sample
size of the presented studies, might limit the test set variability, and not render a
generalizable model from medical practice where big domain shifts can be present
given the subjectiveness of the questionnaires.

CHAPTER 4
Data Description
This chapter provides a general description of all data used in this study. The chapter
includes the diﬀerent required considerations to properly ﬁlter each dataset and obtain
a complete uniﬁed dataset. Moreover, a statistical description of the uniﬁed dataset
is presented.
4.1
Data Description
Data diversity is a prerequisite to guarantee more discriminative information for a
ML algorithm, where the sample distribution can better approximate the population
distribution, achieving an accurate data inference [51]. Suﬃcient data diversity was
ensured with the inclusion of four diﬀerent studies, where ASQs and PSGs data were
collected.
Four diﬀerent studies were conducted at the Stanford Sleep Clinic.
• STAGES
• Airweave
• Balance
• Reﬂex
All studies have worked with Stanford University IRB to ensure that the data pro-
duced may be shared through the Stanford Center for Sleep Sciences and Medicine
with the signed consent of each patient.
Nocturnal PSG in the Stanford Sleep Clinic (SSC) is recorded from patients with
a wide range of sleep disorders. PSGs in the SSC were recorded using a bandpass
pre-ﬁlter with cut-oﬀfrequencies of 0.1 Hz and 0.45 times the sampling frequency.
Since the ASQ is an online questionnaire, it appoints a unique survey identiﬁer
once submitted by a patient. If a patient submits multiple ASQs, the dataset will
contain diﬀerent ASQs for the same patient with diﬀerent survey identiﬁers, resulting
in the duplicates of patients with diﬀerent answered ASQs. Moreover, a patient may
==PAGE:48==
20
4 Data Description
submit the ASQ without completing the full questionnaire - the progress is recorded
in one of the dataset attributes. Conversely, the AHI is a one-time measure per pa-
tient through the nocturnal PSG.
Soon after the selection of only completed ASQs. To properly couple the ASQ
with the correct AHI, besides the patient identiﬁer. The submission date of the ASQs
and the PSG date of the study is considered. When multiple ASQs are present for
the same patient, the ASQ with the nearest date of the PSG study is selected. The
complete pipeline for the dataset selection is presented in Figure 4.1. The studies
STAGES, Ariweave, Balance, and Reﬂex are consequently structured with 1435, 394,
832, and 539 unique patients, respectively. Of 4884 subjects, 3200 (65.52%) remained
after the dataset selection.
Figure 4.1: Data reduction pipeline for the four datasets: STAGES, Airweave, Bal-
ance, and Reﬂex. Diagram (a) depicts the dimensions of the raw datasets. Diagram
(b) depicts the dimensions of the resulting dataset after only completed ASQ are
selected. Diagram (c) depicts the ﬁnal dimension of the datasets after the multiple
ASQs for the same patients are coupled with the nearest AHI’s date diﬀerence.
The uniﬁcation of multiple cohorts can induce skewness in patients based on the
distributions of features [52]. Moreover, when working with disease-related datasets,
prior knowledge of the main demographics distribution can facilitate the inference
of how the target and predictors will be distributed in the dataset. Diseases such
as OSA have a prevalence based on gender, aﬀecting more men the women due to
pathophysiological diﬀerences [53]. Obesity has long been known to be associated
with OSA, as BMI positively correlates with the severity of the disease [4]. Moreover,
the skewness may aﬀect the target distribution (AHI) and bias the model, limiting
the re-usability [52]. Consequently, Table 4.1 presents the distribution of age, gender,
BMI, AHI, and the number of samples of each dataset after the pipeline presented in
Figure 4.1 is applied.

4.2
Unified Dataset
Contrary to STAGES, the studies Airweave, Balance, and Reﬂex were implemented
with an updated ASQ version (4.0). Consequently, before the uniﬁcation of the tab-
ular datasets, all the questions (columns) must be identical.
Six questions (diet_0410, diet_0415, diet_0420, diet_0425, diet_0430, diet_0435)
not present in STAGES were identiﬁed and removed from the Ariweave, Balance and
Reﬂex studies. Questions mdhx_6110, par_0401, par_0920, sched_2220, slpy_0120,
slpy_0220, slpy_0320 were depreciated after the STAGES studies, thus removed from
Ariweave, Balance and Reﬂex studies.
Once all columns from the four datasets presented the same type of questions.
The uniﬁcation was implemented and resulted in a uniﬁed tabular dataset composed
of 3200 completed ASQs with coupled AHIs (rows), and 485 questions (columns) ex-
cluding the subject identiﬁer and target.
Questions presented in the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire are listed in Appendix
7.21, where “Question Name” are the columns of the dataset and the “Numeric Scor-
ing” are the diﬀerent options the user can provide. All the selected questions accept
numerical inputs, ranging from categorical and numerical data types. The column
“Question Name (Abbreviated)” provides a brief description of the question.
Because of the branching logic of the ASQ not all questions are presented to the
subject. For e.g. a subject who answers question mdhx_6710 with answer option four,
indicating the presence of autoimmune diseases then, the question mdhx_6740 will be
presented to further inquire the type of autoimmune disease, else mdhx_6740 will be
automatically ﬁlled with a missing value. The majority of ML algorithms are unable
to discriminate missing values [54, 55]. Consequently, important consideration must

Matching the ASQ to the AHI
The ASQ and AHI originate from the diﬀerent datasets for the studies ARIWEAVE,
Balance, and Reﬂex.
Under the PSG studies containing the AHI, records of the
same subject identiﬁer can be present multiple times, but with diﬀerent time dates.
Consequently, multiple AHI measures are present for the repeated subjects. In order
to match the ASQ with the correct AHI. The dates of the ASQ’s unique subjects and
AHI for a given subject are compared, and the PSG studies with the nearest time
date to the completion of the ASQ are selected as matching AHI.

CHAPTER 5
Data Wrangling &
Feature Selection
This chapter is dedicated to the ASQ dataset adaptation for a ML model. The ASQ
contains more than 400 questions, making the model prone to the curse of dimension-
ality [56]. Also, the branching of the ASQ introduces numerous missing values that
are not discriminate values for ML models. Once the ASQ is properly adapted for
a ML model. The most signiﬁcant predictors must be selected, with domain knowl-
edge and with mathematical models as Analysis of variance (ANOVA) and mutual
information (MI).
5.1
Introduction
Because the ASQ is not disease-speciﬁc and attempts to expand the inclusion of all
sleep disorders by reviewing a wide variety of sleep habits and targeting speciﬁc-
related questions. The amalgamation of questions (features) increases with abundant
values. Making the model more prone to the curse of dimensionality and noise.
Moreover, the branching logic of the ASQ ﬁlters future questions based on previ-
ous responses. As all questions must be present in the tabular dataset, non-presented
questions to a subject are marked as missing values. Consequently, the dataset con-
tains missing values of the type missing not at random (MNAR). As the majority
of ML algorithms are unable to discriminate missing values [54, 55]. Missing values
must be managed with an appropriate data cleansing logic while maintaining a proper
logic in the questions’ response values.
Consequently, the feature dimensionality must be reduced with both, domain
knowledge and mathematical methods (e.g. Mutual Information, a test of variance,
etc..). Additionally the proper manipulation of missing values and tabular structure
must be designed for all selected questions for ML implementation.
==PAGE:54==
26
5 Data Wrangling & Feature Selection
5.2
Data Wrangling
As presented in Chapter 4 Section 4.2 Table 4.2, a signiﬁcant amount of questions
(53.59%) have a percentage of answered responses lower than 75% because of the
ASQ’s branching logic. If the missing values (not interpretable by many learning
algorithms) are replaced by a constant (e.g. −1), a reduction in the variance, and
increased bias towards the constant may be induced and/or reduce the accuracy of
the model. Although, the missing values are of the category type MNAR [57] which
might convey information about the branching logic and are nonignorable data. Con-
sequently, there is a structure/pattern in the missing data, and removing the predictor
might result in the loss of information, leaving a bias/variance trade-oﬀin the model.
As means of reducing the presence of missing values, various categorical and ordi-
nal questions where the answer 0 means the patient does not experience the questioned
pathology convey the same meaning as missing values. Consequently for the type of
questions where the answer response 0 represents:
• 0 = No
• 0 = Never
• 0 = Not at all
• 0 = No RLS symptoms
• 0 = Rarely/Never
• 0 = No previous Diagnosis
the missing values were encoded as zeros.
Furthermore, the ASQ contains multiple questionnaires with a ﬁnal score e.g ESS
and map.
A score of 0 means the pathology is not present in the patient based
on the speciﬁc questionnaire.
Consequently the missing values for the question-

Only categorical, numerical, and ordinal data types are selected from the complete
ASQ as predictors, otherwise, the requirement of a natural language processing algo-
rithm would be necessary, and goes outside form the scope of this study. Consequently,
open questions where the patient is inquired to further describe in the written text a
pathology from previous questions are removed.

CPAP machine
“CPAP device” is a general term often used to describe various respiratory devices
that treat sleep apnea during sleep. If a patient utilizes a CPAP device, the symptoms
of sleep apnea will not be manifested through the night. At the moment of the PSG
study for the AHI, the patient is not allowed to utilize the CPAP device. Consequently,
the presence of false positives can be present in the dataset.
Therefore, if the screening question mdhx_300 has a response of 2 (CPAP/BiPAP
type machine) or 8 (Provent nasal device) the subject is removed. All the questions
of the type pap (18) are removed.

Questions dem_0600 and dem_0610 inquire about the height in feet and inches.

dem_height_meters. The redundant question as dem_0100 (birth year) is removed
as dem_0110 already inquires about the patient’s age at the moment of the ques-
tionnaire. Racial, demographic origin, and capacity to interpret English: dem_0900,
dem_0910, dem_1000, dem_1100, dem_1120 were removed, as deemed not signiﬁ-
cant for the AHI classiﬁcation.

5.4
Feature Selection
As the number of features (ℓ) increases with respect to the number of samples (N) in
a ML model, the performance metric of the model is more prone to improvement, but
then as ℓ′ » ℓthe performance metric could even decrease [56, 61]. This phenomenon
is known as the curse of dimensionality.
The revised studies presented in Chapter 3 implement a pertinent feature selection
method to better select a subset of discriminative predictors. Although, the biggest
initial set of questions is 92. Whereas, the ASQ initially provides a set of 700 ques-
tions. To reduce the number of non-signiﬁcant predictors, an initial data cleansing
can be implemented with the use of domain knowledge.

tion method to better select a subset of discriminative predictors.

biggest initial set of questions is 92. Whereas, the ASQ initially provides a set of 700
questions. To reduce the number of non-signiﬁcant predictors, an initial manual data

cleansing is implemented in Section 5.3, after which from the remaining 419 predic-

The Feature selection algorithms are implemented with the use of the scikit-learn
[62] library in Python programming language [63].
5.4.1
ANOVA Feature Selection
ANOVA is a statistical estimation procedure to determine if there is a statistically sig-
niﬁcant diﬀerence between two categorical groups by testing for diﬀerences in means
using a variance. Consequently, the two testing hypotheses are:
H0 : Means of all groups are equal
H1 : At least one mean of the groups are diﬀerent
Variances quantify the deviation of a distribution from the mean value. In ML
implementations, the variance of predictors can reﬂect the prediction impact on the
response variables (target). Low variances imply no impact from the predictor on the
response. ANOVA considers only linear dependency
The assumptions of the ANOVA test are: (i) subjects are independent, (ii) equal
sample size, (iii) dependent variables are normally distributed, and (iv) Homoscedas-
ticity (similar variances between the groups). Uneven variances in samples result in
biased and skewed test results [64].
If these assumptions are not true for a given set of data, it may still be possible
to use the Kruskal-Wallis H-test, although with some loss of power [62].
The one-way ANOVA test uses the F-ratio to check for variance signiﬁcance be-
tween the groups and is deﬁned by
F =
χ2

n1−1

n2−1

with n the sample size, χ2 the Chi distribution, and n1,n2 the respective degrees of
freedom. Where the degrees of freedoms are deﬁned as n = N −1 with N the sample
size.
Substituting in equation 5.4.1 with the χ2 = n−1 S2
σ2 distribution. Where S2 is the sum
of squares, and σ2 is the variance.

5.4.2
Mutual Information
mutual information (MI) is a measure from information theory [65], and measures
the dependencies between two random variables M(X ,Y ) in accordance with a joint
probability density µ(x, y). In contrast to ANOVA, MI works on the entropy of the
variables and is sensitive to dependencies not manifested in the covariance between
two random variables. Moreover, MI results as zero if the two random variables are
strictly independent. Otherwise, the MI between two random variables will be sym-
metric and non-negative [66].
MI might be viewed as
MI(predictor,target) = Entropy(predictor)−Entropy(predictor|predictor)
and it fall in the 0 < MI < ∞range.
The higher the value, the more information
gain between the predictor and the target. A MI≈0.01 suggest no information gain
between the predictor and the target.
From a set of ℓbivariate predictors, zi = (xi, yi)
i = 1,...,ℓ, which are assume to
be iid realizations of a random variable Z = (X ,Y ) with density µ(x, y), the MI can be
commonly approximated partitioning the supports of X and Y into bins of ﬁnite size
as
I(X ,Y ) ≈
X
i j
p(i, j)log
p(i, j)
px(i)py(j)
(5.4)
If nx(i)[ny(j)] is the number of points contain in the ith bin of X [jth bin of Y ], and
n(j, j) the number of points in their intersection.
Then px(i) ≈nx(i)/N, py(j) ≈
ny(j)/N, and p(i, j) ≈n(i, j)/N [66].
The MI estimators implemented in this study rely on nonparametric methods
based on entropy estimation from k-nearest neighbors distances [62], as proposed by
[66, 67]. The distance of the k-nearest neighbors is computed for the space Z with
the maximum norm
||z −z′|| = max{||x −x′|,||y −y′||}
The estimation for MI is then
I(X ,Y ) = ψ(k)−〈ψ(nx +1)+ψ(ny +1)〉+ψ(N)
(5.5)
with ψ(x) = Γ(x)−1dΓ(x)/dx the digamma function [66]. The algorithm is more data
eﬃcient, adaptive (higher resolution where data are more numerous), and has min-
imal bias. In contrast to conventional estimators based on binnings. The features
that are selected maximize the input-output MI.
Figure 5.2 depicts the implementation of the MI algorithm on a subset of the
dataset dedicated for training (Dtrain). Both genders are considered, and the response
is a four-class problem.

5.4.3
Feature Importance in Gradient Boosting
As it will be later presented in Section 6. The implementation of an ensemble of
decision tree methods with gradient boosting is also advantageous, as the model is
capable of providing estimates of feature importance from a trained predictive model.
The importance of a predictor under a decision tree model is given by the con-
struction of the boosted decision trees within the model. The more purity is gained
after a split of a predictor, the more signiﬁcance the predictor will have for the model.
The purity in the context of gradient boosting, are regression trees (minimize squared
error greedily) ﬁt to the Hessian gradients of the loss function. Five diﬀerent mea-
sures are computed by the XGB model, and they convey the following information:
• Weightt: the number of times a feature is used to split the data across all
trees.
• Gaint: the average gain across all splits the feature is used in.
• Cover: the average coverage across all splits the feature is used in.
• Total gain: the total gain across all splits the feature is used in.
• Total cover: the total coverage across all splits the feature is used in.
A visualization of the most signiﬁcant features selected by a baseline XGB model
can be observed in Figure 5.3. The higher the score, the more signiﬁcant the feature
under the speciﬁc metric.
Although, the nature of the predictors as binary or continuous can heavily weigh
on the feature importance. As an example, the predictor age might be highly corre-
lated to the response, so the predictors will be used at most once in each tree. In
contrast, age, as a continuous variable might be utilized more often on diﬀerent levels
of the tree, because continuous variables have a higher number of possible variables
for a split.
==PAGE:69==
5.4 Feature Selection
41
Figure 5.3: The ﬁve diﬀerent metrics are given after the training of an XGB model.
The higher the score, the more signiﬁcant the feature under the speciﬁc metric.
==PAGE:70==
42
5 Data Wrangling & Feature Selection
5.5
Conclusion
The pre-processing data cleansing pipeline for removing missing values with the most
suitable response signiﬁcantly reduced the occurrences of missing values. Moreover,
the appropriate data transformation to questions as in the demographic, RLS, and
Schedule section successfully reduces the nuisance factors among the predictors. Ad-
ditionally, the data inspection reduced the dataset to 419 predictors.
The use of a feature selection algorithm can underlay statistical relations between
the predictors and the response. The underlying relation will be highly dependent on
the selected method. While ANOVA selects the predictors by studying the variance
of the predictor and the response. MI uses the joint probability with a k-nearest
neighbor fashion. Lately, XGB, at the end of each training round, can provide the
most signiﬁcant predictors based on ﬁve diﬀerent measures. Some predictors can be
highly scored by more than one measure, while others might be scored only once. An
understating of the data type inﬂuence in the model helps for the proper discrimina-
tion of signiﬁcant predictors.
To retain the most signiﬁcant predictors based on the MI and ANOVA score, it
is observable from Figure 5.2, and Figure 5.1, that the scores start to converge after
the 30th predictor. Moreover, the most signiﬁcant predictors go in accordance with
the medical literature, view 7.21 for the predictors deﬁnition. Therefore, the 40 most
signiﬁcant predictors are selected to train the model.
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 6
Learning Algorithm
This chapter introduces the methods behind the machine learning algorithm imple-
mented by eXtreme Gradient Boosting (XGB). The concepts are applied to the dataset
and evaluated in order to select the most suitable optimization conﬁguration based on
the dataset properties.
6.1
Introduction
The term “learning” with accumulated experiences in computer science and mathe-
matics closely relates to the ability of an algorithm to generalize. The goal of statisti-
cal learning and pattern recognition is to discover patterns in data that will eﬀectively
generalize to unobserved data by establishing a relationship without the use of heuris-
tics [68, 69].
A ﬁeld within computer science, ML.
Enables the implementation of learning
algorithms with the use of fast computations to unravel the underlying relationship
of complex problems from the big datasets [70]. The majority of learning algorithms
are branched into two diﬀerent categories: (i) supervise learning, (ii) unsupervised
learning. In supervised learning, initial training data is available and utilized by the
algorithm to exploit the data as a priori has known information. Conversely, un-
der the branch of unsupervised learning, prioir knowledge is not available and the
algorithm works on the cluster identiﬁcation based generally, on similarity measures
between the observations [68]. In this Thesis, we will utilize supervised learning algo-
rithms.
In this project, the classiﬁcation task is concerned with mapping from a set of
predictors X from the ASQ into the predicted response Y (AHI) by learning some
function f . The mapping is done with the use of decision trees. Converging decision
rules to split the samples into vertices and branches for a class assignment.
6.1.1
Supervised Learning Task
A predictive model model is build to predict the response variable Y ∈Y characterize
by the class label or targets, using a set of covariates Xi = (X1,..., Xm) ∈X denoted by
==PAGE:72==
44
6 Learning Algorithm
the predictors. Given a training dataset
Dtrain = {(Y1, X1),(Y2,X2),...,(YL, XL)}
assumed to be independent and identically distributed random variables (i.i.d) of
sample size L from a joint distribution PY,X .
The task of building a predictive model is designed under the framework of sta-
tistical learning theory [71]. Where, a predictive function f is estimated to learn a
mapping X 7−→Y using Dtrain and Dval [69]. To predict how well the model may
generalize to unseen data, the constructed classiﬁer is checked against a test set
Dtest =
©
XL+1,..., Xl+U
ª, for which labels are not provided.
Furthermore, successively the ﬁtted model is used to predict Y . A validation set Dval =
{XL+U+1,..., XL+U+V } provides an unbiased evaluation of the model on Dval while tuning
the model’s hyperparameters. For an unbiased learning, the Dtrain
TDval
TDtest = ;
must be disjoint. The total number of data items can be denoted as N=L+V+U.
From N=3200 ASQs, the L, V, and U splits are constructed by utilizing the 70%,
20%, and 10% of N; respectively.
6.1.2
The Model
Initially, to make a prediction depending on the predictors X, a model f is utilized
f : X →A
and maps every input x ∈X to a corresponding prediction a ∈A, The model, under
the tree-based method may be viewed as decision rules or decision functions.
With AX the set of all functions mapping from X to A. To consider the best
model estimate ˆf from AX it is necessary to determine the best parameters under
the Dtrain and Dval [72, 73]. Consequently, to restrict the model class F to the total
function space F ⊂AX , the model will learn some parameters θ ∈Θ adjusted to the
Dtrain and Dval, the best model estimate may then be view as
ˆf (x) = f (x; ˆθ)
To best estimate ˆf with a tree model, considerable approximations must be made, as
tree models are linear functions [73].
6.1.3
Measure The Prediction
To quantify the quality of how well ˆf approximates the responses an optimization
problem is annexed to the learning process. Abstractly, these can be written as
θ∗∈argmin

derivatives and accumulate the information gathered from previous iterations [74].
The loss will then measure the discrepancy between the predicted responses and the
true responses [73].
However, the true outcome still is unknown for the prediction, and the loss is
consequently a random variable L(Y,a). To estimate an overall error measure, the
expected value of the loss function is considered on each individual set.

The error computes an average loss of each set. The approach implemented in this

and the responses. Once the constraints are deﬁned, the model is validated on the
Dval, where the hyperparameters are tuned to minimize/maximize the Eval. Once the
best Eval is found, the model with the best hydrometers is tested on the Dtest.
6.1.4
Evaluation Metrics
During model training, the objective function provides the gradient information, both
ﬁrst and second-order gradient (in the situation of XGB), based on model predictions
and observed responses. The evaluation metric is then monitor on the Dtrain and
Deval. The convergence of the metric function is an indication on how well the model
selects the best learnable variables Θ from the observed data Dtrain to generalize on
the Deval. The main objective is to minimize equation 6.1, and arrive at a global
minimum.
For a classiﬁcation problem, where the response is a ﬁnite set of discrete variables
Y = yi for some i ∈Nn. Various loss functions can be implemented as evaluation met-
rics.
The cross-entropy loss function is deﬁned as the negative log-likelihood of a logistic
model that returns the probability of a prediction to be of a speciﬁc class. For a binary
classiﬁcation where the response y ∈{0,1} and a probability estimate ˆy, the log loss

6.1.5
Bias-variance trade-off
Since our goal is prediction how well the model performs on independent data from
PY,X . If the model is too ﬂexible it may ﬁt too well the Dtrain and will not generalize.
This behavior is known as overﬁtting, leading to poor generalization performance.
Conversely, if the model is not ﬂexible enough the model will then underﬁt the Dtrain
(high error). This trade-oﬀfor the model complexity is known as the bias-variance
tradeoﬀ.
When the model class F is restricted with the Dtrain and Dval sets for a ﬁtting pro-
cedure the distribution of θ is induced and deﬁnes the constrains from the observable
data.
==PAGE:75==
6.1 Introduction
47
Figure 6.1: Error in training and testing with high bias and variance.
From Figure 6.1.5 it is observable how the validation error decreases with the
model’s complexity. However, the test error (unseen data) increases dramatically, as
the model is not able to generalize. Additionally, the error can be decomposed into
two terms, bias and variance. It is observable how a high bias induces high error for
validation and testing. Moreover, with a high variance low generalization is observ-
able. The tradeoﬀthen seeks to design the model at the lowest error for training and
testing.
The implementation of the three splits is a method to improve the model’s gen-
eralization and helps to control the bias-variance tradeoﬀ. Diﬀerent techniques to
improve the bias-variance tradeoﬀare present and known as regularization methods
[73, 75].
6.1.6
Complexity Measures & Penalization
There is no universal way to deﬁne complexity [76, 73], although the objective of the
measure can be deﬁned as
Ω: F →R+
The complexity measure can be a function of f , where the most common measure is
Least Absolute Shrinkage and Selection Operator (LASSO) regression (ℓ1-norm), the
quadratic regularizer Ridge Regression (ℓ2-norm) , and ℓ0-norm which in the case of
regression measure the number of non-zero coeﬃcients [77].
Overﬁtting occurs when the model achieve almost perfect loss reduction/max-
imization, but when presented to new data (Dval, Dtest) the performance worsen
signiﬁcantly. Complex model, long training and noise in the Dtrain causes “memoriza-
tion” the noise and ﬁts too closely to the Dtrain, and the model is unable to generalize
==PAGE:76==
48
6 Learning Algorithm
e.g correctly classify the AHI in the Dtest.
To control the over-ﬁtting, as with Ridge and LASSO. The cost function 6.1.3 is
altered in order to have a stronger preference for small weights, leading to a modiﬁed
cost function of the form
E(f ) = ˆE(f )+λθ(f )
(6.6)
where θ(f ) measures the model complexity, and the hyperparameter λ controls how
strong the complexity is penalized. The training error will thus always select low
values of λ, consequently, λ must not be tuned from the training error, but rather
the validation error, or through cross-validation.
LASSO is known as a shrinkage technique as it shrinks the less important predic-
tors coeﬃcients to zero reducing the model’s complexity and multicollinearity. The
shrinkage is done by utilizing the absolute sum of the coeﬃcients.
λθ(f ) = λ
X

| ˆθj |
By contrast, Ridge penalizes for the sum of the squared value of the coeﬃcients. The
penalization aﬀects bigger weights and results in more evenly distributed coeﬃcients.
λθ(f ) = λ

6.1.7
Curse of Dimensionality
The curse of dimensionality, ﬁrst presented by Bellman [56], refers to the phenomena
that occur when high dimensional data is utilized to predict a response.
As the
number of features (m) increases with respect to the number of observations (N).
The model’s performance metric is more prone to improvement, but then as m′ » m
the performance metric could even decrease [56, 61]. Each predictor increases the
data set requirement exponentially (AX , view Section 6.1.2). Additionally, with the
increase in dimensions, there are more chances for the occurrence of multicollinearity.
6.2
Extreme Gradient Boosting
eXtreme Gradient Boosting (XGB) [78] is an open-source library to extract patterns
from date with a ML methodology. The algorithm uses an ensemble of weak learn-
ers, especially the tree ensemble boosting method to create a strong classiﬁer or
regression-based model. XGB has empirically proven to be highly eﬀective for numer-
ous problems involving ML, achieving top scores in comparison to other algorithms
applied to the same dataset as it can be observed in the world’s largest community
of ML competitors,Kaggle1.
1https://www.kaggle.com/search?q=xgboost+in%3Acompetitions

6.2.2
Tree Methods
Tree methods are linear, interpretable models which can be visualized as a “tree”
structure that can partition the feature space X into a set of T rectangular, non-
overlapping regions R1,...,RT and ﬁt a constant model in each region. Figure 6.2.2
provides an example of how a simple tree method partitions the feature isq_0200 to
classify the AHI as a binary class.
The three methods commence with a top root node, were the class of the avail-
able samples are identiﬁed. In the provided example of Figure 6.2.2, the root node
contains 1638 observations from the class “Normal_mild” and 665 from the class
“Moderate_severe”. The node is labeled with the majority voting of the classes. Sub-
sequently, the node branches to nodes below named splits. The nodes at the bottom
are named terminal nodes. The tree model can be written as

6.2.4
Boosting
Boosting algorithms are built under the assumption that an ensemble of weak learners
- deﬁned by base functions ϕm(x), may provide a stronger learner. As a strong learners,
we intend to have a high probability to output a hypothesis that is correct on all but an
arbitrarily small fraction of the instance [81]. As the ensemble model is a combination
(sum) of weak hypotheses, boosting may also reduce the bias and variance of the ﬁnal

where g ′(xx) indicates the gradient as the
slope of most rapid decent, and g ′′(xk)
the Hessian, representing the curvature
of the function.
Newton’s method uti-
lizes the curvature information to take a more direct route.

6.4
Regularization
The regularization objective is to reduce the complexity of the model. As presented
in Section 6.1.6 complexity is a way to control overﬁtting. In the scheme of basis
function expansion. Complexity is dependent on the tree depth and the number of
terminal nodes.
Complexity can also depend on the dimension of the boundary decisions with
regions R1,...,RT . An increase in regions induces regions that can ﬁt small local struc-
tures, and thus are more complex.
For ﬁtting additive tree models, XGB penalizes the leaf weights ω1,...,ωT com-
plexity. A tree with all equal weights can be considered a constant model, as the
weights diﬀer, the tree model increases in complexity.
==PAGE:87==
6.4 Regularization
59
Additionally, a constraint on the minimum number of observations on each termi-
nal node, can limit the shrinkage of the regions. This will limit the variance as more
observations will be required to estimate the leaf weights [73].
6.4.1
Complexity Penalization
XGB complexity penalization is presented in the equation 6.20

• λ: l2 regularization of the leaf weights
• α: l1 regularization of the leaf weights

6.4.2
Pruning
Let us recall that Gain implies the relative contribution of a predictor to the tree
model. A higher Gain implies it is more important for generating a prediction with
a split.
As a tree continues to grow in depth, the gain will be minimized after each split.
The l1 and l2 regularization parameters will reduce the gain to a negative value. If
the growth is limited to the negative gain, potential splits further down might have
positive gains which account for the negative gain further up.
To prevent an early growth of the tree depth, XGB implements a method named
pruning. Trees initially grow to a maximum depth (hyperparameter), once reached.
From a bottom-up direction, nodes that lead to negative gains are removed.
6.4.3
Randomization
Row Subsampling
Friedman (2002) proposed stochastic gradient boosting as an extension of gradient
booting [83]. Whereas means to improve generalization, and increase the variance
between trees.
The algorithm, found in XGB, implements a hyperparameter row
subsampling 0 < ωr 1 at each boosting iteration. It denotes the fraction of observations
to be randomly sampled for each tree without replacement. The models are then
combined with an average operator to form the ensemble model. The average tends
to reduce the variance of the ensemble model.
Column Subsampling
Similarly to Random Forest implementation by Breinman (2001) [84]. XGB imple-
ments a predictor subsampling hyperparameter 0 < ωc1 to induce a random selection
on the predictor selection, with a subset selection each time a split is considered. The
averaging for the ensemble model further reduces the variance.
==PAGE:89==
6.5 Bayesian Hyperparameter Optimization
61
6.4.4
Missing values
Tree models, with the inclusion of XGB, are capable to process missing values during
training. XGB implements a default direction every time a missing value is found
in a split. Each node branches into left and right. The branch which minimizes the
objective is learned from the data.
6.5
Bayesian Hyperparameter Optimization
The search for the best model hyperparameters is one of the most ponderous tasks.
hyperparameters are not estimable from data, but rather tuned by inspection. As
the complexity of a model grows, more combinations of hyperparameters must be
considered simultaneously in the search for the best conﬁguration.
Grid search is one of the most common algorithms for hyperparameter optimiza-
tion. With grid search, a model is built for every combination of various hyperpa-
rameters. Once evaluated, The model which results in the best evaluation metric is
selected. As the number of hyperparameters grows, the models to evaluate become
computational and time expensive. Moreover, the selection of the best hyperparame-
ters is limited to a user-deﬁned range.
Random search, improves grid search with a random selection of hyperparameters
to ﬁnd the best solution. Although, yields high variance during computing. Since the
selection of parameters is completely random. Figure 6.5 depicts a set of hyperparam-
eters, and how grid search and random search iterate over the deﬁned hyperparameter
in order to ﬁnd the best model. A drawback of both is the lack of pas information
for a better selection of hyperparameters.
Figure 6.6: Comparison between (a) grid search; and (b) random search for hy-
perparameter tuning. The black points denote the hyperparameter candidates. The
upper distribution depicts the metric performance, higher is better.
==PAGE:90==
62
6 Learning Algorithm
Consequently, the choice of parameter sampling can highly inﬂuence the best se-
lection of hyperparameter. In this study, the Optuna library implements an eﬃcient
sampling and pruning algorithm with the utilization of bayesian model-based opti-
mization [2].
Bayesian Optimization
Bayesian optimization is a tool to optimize costly objective functions with slow eval-
uation time [85]. Consider the problem where the hyperparameter optimization is
represented as
xopt = argminx∈X f (x)
Here Here f (x) represents an objective score to minimize, with f : X →R, and xopt
is the set of hyperparameters that yields the lowest score. Ensemble models, as with
XGB result in a complex ﬁnal model.
Bayesian Optimization builds a probabilistic model for the objective function,
called the surrogate function.
That maps input values to a probability of a loss.
Observed results yi = f (xi), are subsequently sample to the set D = {(x1, y1),...,(xi, yi)},
which is used to update the next suggestion. From Byes, where P(A|B) = P(B|A)P(A)
resembles the deﬁnition of posterior equals the likelihood time the prior. Then
P(f |D) = P(D|f )P(f )
with P(f ) the prior of the objective function, and P(D|f ) as the likelihood dependent
on the amount of observed results. With this approximation, the posterior probability
is a surrogate objective function
The surrogate function yields a response surface as presented in Figure 6.5.
==PAGE:91==
6.5 Bayesian Hyperparameter Optimization
63
Figure 6.7: Response surface for AdaBoost algorithm 2
The surrogates are simpler in computational demands. The Bayesian method will
iteratively optimize the surrogate, and then evaluate the best selection of hyperpa-
rameters on the true objective function [85].
To select the next set of hyperparameters from the surrogate function. The ac-
quisition function S explores areas in the objective space known to previously yield
favorable results. It can be deﬁned as the Expected Improvement
EIy∗(x) =
Zy∗
−∞
(y∗−y)p(y|x)d y
(6.23)
with y∗a threshold value of the objective function, y the true responses, x the pro-
posed set of hyperparameters, and p(y|x) is the surrogate. The maximization of EIy∗
with respect to x will yield the best hyperparameters under the surrogate function
[85].
This study implements the Tree-structured Parzen Estimator proposed [85] as a
surrogate function. The algorithm applies hte Bayes rule to the surrogate:
p(y|x) = p(x|y)p(y)
p(x)
with p(x|y) the probability of the hyperparameters given the score on the objective
function. It creates two hierarchical processes l(x) and g(x) as generative models to
model the domain variables when the objective function surpasses a speciﬁc quantile
y∗.
p(x|y) =
(
l(x)
ify < y∗
g(x)
ify ≥y∗
==PAGE:92==
64
6 Learning Algorithm
l(x) and g(x) can be seen as a distributions for the hyperparameters. With l(x) con-
tains values of the objective functions smaller than the y∗. Conversely, g(x) contains
values the objective functions greater than y∗. Consequently, the algorithm will select
hyperparameter which are more likely under l(x) than under g(x).
6.6
Methods
This section described the explorative approach taken on the ASQ for the prediction
of AHI in a classiﬁcation problem, with the use of XGB as a learning algorithm.
6.6.1
Model Design
OSA subjective symptoms are diﬀerently manifested in the population.
Various
population-based studies have shown that OSA is more frequent in men than in
women [4, 53]. Therefore, an attempt to highlight this discriminative characteristics
and obtain a model with higher speciﬁcity is tested by training and testing a model
with the inclusion of all subjects, only male, and only female.
6.6.2
Data Partitioning
After the implementation of the methods described in Section 5, where signiﬁcant
predictors were selected with domain knowledge, and subjects with the satisfying cri-
teria were included. A ﬁnal dataset of 2800 observations and 419 predictors ready for
a ML learning algorithm was utilized for the study.
The dataset was split into a Dtrain (70%), Dval (20%) and Dtest set (10%). In
Section 4, we discussed the presence of imbalance classes for AHI, with a skewed
distribution towards the “normal” and “mild” classes. To avoid splits without the
presence of the less frequent classes. The data splits were done in a stratiﬁed fashion
implemented with the Sklearn-Library [62], where the relative class frequencies are
approximately preserved in each split with respect to the class labels.
6.6.3
Model Validation & Testing
A set of the diﬀerent performance metrics is used to evaluate the model. Each metric
provides a diﬀerent insight into the model performance in the Dtest.
To test the performance in a binary or multi-class problem, and identify how
well the model performs on the prediction of each class during Dtest. The confusion
==PAGE:93==
6.6 Methods
65
matrix is utilized, where each row corresponds to the true class and the columns to
the predicted instances. The confusion matrix conveys the following measures:
• True Positive (TP): number of predictions where the classiﬁer correctly pre-
dicts the positive class as positive.
• True Negative (TN): number of predictions where the classiﬁer correctly
predicts the negative class as negative.
• False Positive (FP): number of predictions where the classiﬁer incorrectly
predicts the negative class as positive.
• False Negative (FN): number of predictions where the classiﬁer incorrectly
predicts the positive class as negative.
In the situation of a four-class problem. The encoding of the response is from
continues to categorical is done as follows

The Precision will
indicate the fraction of predictions that were predicted as true positive ˆyi,k = yi,k.
It is calculated by dividing the true positives by anything that was predicted as a
positive. The Recall /TPR/Sensitivity indicates the fraction of all positive samples
that were correctly predicted as positive. It is calculated by dividing the true positives
by anything that should have been predicted as positive.

(average), give equal weight to precision and recall.

6.6.4
Validation & Testing
Early stop is a form of regularization to avoid overﬁtting the training set. It uses
a validation set to compute the loss function at the end of each iteration. The loss
is monitored on the validation set. Once the validation loss starts to increase (mini-
mization problem), the training is stop as the model generalization ability starts to
decrease. On this study the Dval, comprised by 20% of the selected D was utilized
for two purposes.
==PAGE:95==
6.6 Methods
67
Firstly, an initial baseline model is trained with the complete Dtrain, and after
each iteration the model is tested with the Dval to provide an unbiased performance
measure. Then, the early stop stop criteria is confronted. Thereby tracking the per-
formance as training progresses, and avoid overﬁtting Dtrain.
Secondly, Dval is utilized to search the best hyperparameter for the model Op-
tional formulates the hyperparameter optimization as a process of minimizing/maxi-
mizing an objective function and returns a validation score [2]. On each Optuna study,
a model is trained and the AUC cost is computed. After each study the Bayesian
Optimization will search the best hyperparameters based on the predictions made
from the Dval.
The Dtest set is comprised of by 10% of the selected D, and is used to provide an
unbiased estimate of the models performance. Furthermore, the performance on the
diﬀerent classes, discriminated by gender was investigated. This analysis gives insight
if OSA symptoms are manifested diﬀerently for the male and female population in
the ASQ.
Hyperparameter Search
The set of hyperparameter posteriors distribution given to Optuna are presented
in Table 6.1. Where α, and λ are the l1, and l2 regularization terms on weights,
respectively. γ is the minimum loss reduction required to make a further partition on
a leaf node of the tree. Max depth is the maximum depth of a tree. The more trees,
the more complex the ensemble of the model will be, thus more prone to overﬁtting.
Colsample bytree is the random regularization term to randomly sample a portion of
the predictors from the data when constructing each tree. Max bin is the maximum
number of discrete bins to bucket continuous features [1]. Colsample by node is the
subsample ratio of predictors for each split (Columns Subsampling), min child weight
is the minimum sum of instance weight (hessian) needed in a child, base score is
the initial global bias, used in the initial prediction score of all instances. When the
hessian is neatly zero, the η regularization term will have no eﬀect as the heassian is
in the denominator. Consequently, the hyperparameter Max delta steps introduce an
“absolute” regularization, capping the weight before applying the η hyperparameter.

To quantify the importance of both single hyperparameters and of interactions
between hyperparameters. An ANOVA study as suggested by [87]. Given a study,
ANOVA ﬁts a random forest regression model that predicts the objective value given
a parameter conﬁguration. The more accurate this model is, the more reliable the
importances assessed by this class are [2].

after the 1000 iteration. Consequently, the number of iterations was deﬁned at 100000
for all proposed models.

The confusion matrix for the Dtrain and Dtest is presented in Figure 6.12. The

the loss metrics. The model did a better ﬁt in training and demonstrated low gener-
alization performance. Moreover, the generalization performance is observable with
the weighted accuracy from the Dtrain and Dtest, which is 62.1% and 44%, respectively.
In the learning stages, the model demonstrated a diﬃculty to correctly classify the
moderate cases as moderate, with 155 moderate cases predicted as mild, and 97 as
normal. For the Dtest, the prediction are inferior than random guessing. The Preci-
sion, Recall, and F-score were 0.498, 0.446, and 0.456, respectively. The AUC for the
Dtrain and Dtest is of 0.739, and 0.629, respectively.
Optimized
The optimization stage was set with 5000 trials and 20 random points for each study.
A slice plot is presented in Figure 6.13. Each panel is a hyperparameter for tuning.
The x-axis indicates the posteriors given in Table 6.1. The y-axis indicates the loss
value on each trial, and the z-axis indicates the trial number as a heat map over the
trial points. Because the selected objective function is the AUC, hyperparameters
that maximize the objective functions are selected. The best AUC on the Dval is
obtained with η = 1.317e−6 α = 11.0, λ = 5.6, and max depth = 6. With an increase in
α and λ a more regularized model might be obtained, and better predictions on the
Dval can be produced. The learning rate η is decreased, suggesting a slower learner.
The hyperparameter distributions indicates how a 0.4 <base score< 0.6 , 0.4 <colsample
by node< 0.6, a 0.9 <colsample by tree< 1, a small η ≈10−6, max depth≈7, a γ < 5, a
min child weight< 0.6, a 10 < α < 15, a 4 < λ < 6, and a 0.6 <subsample< 0.8.

dictors. With max delta step in the situation of unbalanced observations in a split,
the Hessian can tend to zero. As demonstrated in equation 6.14, the Heassian is at
the denominator. Consequently, max delta step introduce a regularization capping
the weight before the next η step, making the weight smaller instead of tending to
inﬁnite. Noise and non highly signiﬁcant features might be the factors on why the
random samplings of predictors aﬀects the model’s performance.
The regularization parameters α and λ impacted less signiﬁcantly the optimization.
From the left panel from Figure 6.14. It is observable how the optimization via the
surrogate plateaus after the 1000th trial, with a prior minimal loss increase in the
order of 10−2.
